{London, Dulwich College MS XXII, fols. 81v-85v: La Estorie del Euangelie}\n

{=Sample represents all the text in English in this hand=}\n
{=Script - proto-Gothic book hand=}\n
{=Status - MS punctuation done; tagging notes and textual notes up to date=}\n
{=Each stanza (mostly, but not all, quatrains) was supposed to start\n
with a somewhat larger or more ornate initial capital. These initials\n
are often missing. If the scribe\'s guide letter is visible it is\n
incorporated between insertion arrows. The English text was evidently\n
written first and spaces left for the Latin quotations which were\n
added afterwards, the spaces often not matching the size of the text.\n
Overflow text has to be fitted into marginal spaces=}\n
{~f81va~}\n

# 2000\n
{London, Lambeth Palace Library 487, fols 1r-21v, 30v-51v, hand A language 1: Lambeth Homilies I-V, IX-XIII}\n
C13a1(ca1200)\n
372 262 E\n
{=NW Worcs=}\n
{=Sample represents all the text in English in this hand in this kind of\n
language. The same scribe also writes the Poema Morale in the same MS,\n
see text no. 5 (lampmt.tag). The homilies themselves are two rather\n
different kinds of language, this (language 1) being more conservative\n
in some respects than language 2 (see text no. 2001, lamhomA2t.tag).\n
Both types of language seem to be from the same place. The language\n
{of PM marches closely with that of language 2=}\n
{=Script - Protogothic book-hand. The hand is somewhat variable\n
depending on stint and pen-cut. For the first seven and a half folios\n
the scribe uses a form of insular \'g\' that sits on the ruled line and\n
does not have a descender below the line. It is horizontally broad and\n
vertically shortened, made with a head stroke and two opposing curves,\n
very like a broad majuscule \'s\'. This was perhaps influenced by the\n
figura he found in his exemplar for the first two homilies. On fol 8v\n
the scribe seems to start experimenting with the figura, lengthening the\n
final curve somewhat and bringing it a little below the line. On folio\n
9r from the start of Homily III he adopts the form of insular \'g\' that\n
he uses thereafter, made for the first two strokes like a \'t\', with\n
curved stem and head stroke followed by an opposing curved stroke\n
starting at the base line and descending well below it. Note that Orm\n
formed his insular \'g\' in much the same fashion. Wanley, in Hickes\n
Thesaurus (Hickes 1707: 1, plates VI and VII between pages 144 and 145),\n
reproduces (by careful imitation) a piece from fol. 3r and a piece from\n
fol. 21v (in Language 2) with the implication that they represent two\n
different hands (I owe this observation to Malcolm Parkes). I believe,\n
however, that in spite of a certain variability, including the clear\n
change of figura for insular \'g\', that the Homilies are written\n
throughout by the same scribe. As a literatim copyist of spelling\n
systems he may well also have been influenced by his exemplars\' scripts=}\n
{=Original transcript provided by JJS, corrected against microfilm,\n
formatted and tagged by ML=}\n
{=Status - MS punctuation done; tagging notes and textual notes up to date=}\n
{=The scribe has identical shapes for wynn and for \'y\', but almost\n
entirely consistently \'y\' is dotted and wynn is not. Thorn has its own\n
distinct figura, though very occasional thorn for wynn and vice versa\n
suggests that the exemplar\'s script might have had some interchangeability.\n
Note also occasional use of wynn for insular g indicating that the\n
exemplar may also have displayed interchangeability of thorn/wynn with\n
yogh. Some uncertainty (presumably exemplar-induced) is evident in the\n
use of \'i\' and \'r\'. It seems likely that the exemplar\'s figura for \'r\'\n
was very similar to a simple minim stroke. On this see further Sisam\n
(1951: 111). The scribe makes many \'running\' corrections as he goes\n
along. Many are by overwriting mistaken letters with correct ones.\n
Others are by subpuncting and/or crossing through words or letters and\n
interlining others above. I assume in the tagged text that all the\n
corrections have been made by the main scribe himself, so I have adopted\n
them, marked in the usual way. Punctuation marks are punctus, punctus\n
elevatus, the rarer punctus versus (like modern semi-colon and realised\n
as such in the tagged text) and also the punctus interrogativus, which\n
is like a punctus elevatus but with a looped upper stroke. In the tagged\n
text this is realised according to my normal practice with a modern\n
question mark. It is not consistently employed for all questions. Morris\n
(1868) does not differentiate it from punctus elevatus=}\n

{="Then shall the scourge cease". Morris (1868) reads yE ORd and\n

$be/vps23_BId\n
{=Singular form but plural sense. Morris (1868) translates: "and say\n
there what is in his mind" which is a very loose paraphrase and I don\'t\n
think can be right syntactically. It also involves emending HI to HE.\n
Note also that although rarely used in the plural, the form BId is\n
found once for "are" in the Lambeth version of Poema Morale\n
(lampmt.tag) in the same manuscript and hand as this, and also\n
occurs twice in one of the versions of the PM in Egerton 613 - egpm1t.tag=}\n

$gi:tsere/npl_gITC+ER+ES $-er/xs-agt-npl_+ER+ES $/pln_+ES\n
{=Morris (1868) reads gITTERES. This is figurally equally possible\n
because the head stroke of the supposed second T could be seen to have\n
merged with that of the first. But it seems to me that the second of the\n
pair has been formed as a C (which also has a flat top stroke in this\n
scribe\'s hand). This reading also fits what is clearly the intended\n
meaning. Cf. Also the spelling gIETCER+ES in the Trinity text of the\n
Poema Morale (trinpmt.tag)=}\n
{.}\n

{(*BEATI QUI NUnC FLETIS QuoniaM ?RED[ITIS] .(}\n
{=The last word is very faint on the microfilm. The quotation from Luke\n
6:21 should end QUIA RIDEBITIS. Here the abbreviation is certainly for\n
\'quoniam\' not \'quia\'. Morris (1868) reads QuoniaM RIDEBITIS, with no\n
indication of the second word being abbreviated. There are certainly not\n
enough letters to make RIDEBITIS and the first three look to me to be\n
RED. This is perhaps a case of erroneous expansion by the scribe of\n
abbreviated forms in his exemplar=}\n

{=sic - cf. yURgES f3v and yURgEd f8v above for "things" and "seems"\n
(from Yyncan). Morris (1868) notes those miswritings, but here takes\n
yURG to be a form of the verb Yurfen and translates "than any one dare\n
endure". MED does not accept this and has a ghost entry (s.v. thurg)\n
glossed "a pain". It seems clear however that this spelling is a third\n
example of the Lambeth scribe (a literatim copyist) misreading some\n
form of \'ing\' as \'urg\'=}\n

{(*QUI UIUIT &2 *Regnat.(}\n
{\\\\}\n
{199}\n
{London, Lambeth Palace Library 487, fols. 37v-45r, hand A, lang 1: Lambeth Homily X}\n
{ca1200}\n
{372 262}\n
{~f37v~}\n
{=In this homily the Latin phrases and some internal initials are\n
in red. They were almost certainly added afterwards and probably\n
by a different scribe. The main scribe leaves spaces for the Latin\n
phrases but they are often not long enough and in these cases nothing\n
has been written in them. The missing text is supplied here, bracketed\n
in the usual way, from that supplied by Morris (1868)=}\n
{(\'*DE OCTO UICIIS . &2 DE DUODECIM ABUSIUIS HUIus SECULI .\'(}\n
{\\}\n
{=Space has been left for a two-line enlarged initial capital not\n
carried out=}\n
{([o]MNIA NIMIA NOCENT . &2 TEMPerAnCIA \\ MATE*R UIRTUTEM DICITU*R .(}\n

# 2001\n
{London, Lambeth Palace Library 487, fols. 21v-30v, 51v-65r, hand A, language 2: Lambeth Homilies VI-VIII, XIV-XVII}\n
C13a1(ca1200)\n
372 262 W\n
{=NW Worcs=}\n
{=Sample represents all the text in English in this hand in this kind of\n
language. The same scribe also writes the Poema Morale in the same MS,\n
see text no. 5 (lampmt.tag). The homilies themselves are in two rather\n
different kinds of language, language 1 (see text no. 2000, lamhomA1t.tag)\n
being more conservative in some respects than this language (language 2).\n
Both types of language seem to be from the same place. The language of PM\n
marches closely with this language than with language 1=}\n
{=Script - Protogothic book-hand. The hand is somewhat variable\n
depending on stint and pen-cut. See further the notes to lamhomA1t.tag=}\n
{=Original transcript provided by JJS, corrected by ML=}\n
{=Status - MS punctuation done; tagging notes and textual notes up to date=}\n
{=The scribe has identical shapes for wynn and for \'y\', but almost\n
entirely consistently \'y\' is dotted and wynn is not. Thorn has its own\n
distinct figura, though very occasional thorn for wynn and vice versa\n
suggests that the exemplar\'s script might have had some\n
interchangeability. Note also occasional use of wynn for insular g\n
indicating that the exemplar may also have displayed interchangeability\n
of thorn/wynn with yogh. Some uncertainty (presumably exemplar-induced)\n
is evident in the use of \'i\' and \'r\'. It seems likely that the\n
exemplar\'s figura for \'r\' was very similar to a simple minim stroke.\n
On this see further Sisam (1951: 111). The scribe makes many \'running\'\n
corrections as he goes along. Many are by overwriting mistaken letters\n
with correct ones. Others are by subpuncting and/or crossing through\n
words or letters and interlining others above. I assume in the tagged\n
text that all the corrections have been made by the main scribe himself,\n
so I have adopted them, marked in the usual way. Punctuation marks are punctus,\n
punctus elevatus, the rarer punctus versus (like modern semi-colon and\n
realised as such in the tagged text) and also the punctus\n
interrogativus, which is like a punctus elevatus but with a looped upper\n
stroke. In the tagged text this is realised according to my normal\n
practice with a modern question mark. It is not consistently employed\n
for all questions. Morris (1868) does not differentiate it from punctus\n
elevatus=}\n
{195}\n
{London, Lambeth Palace Library 487, fols. 21v-25r, hand A, lang 2: Lambeth Homily VI}\n
{ca1200}\n
{372 262}\n
{=This homily follows straight on from homily V in the same hand but\n
somewhat different language. Homily VI is in verse (rhymed couplets) but\n
written as prose=}\n
{~f21v~}\n
{=Space has been left for a two line enlarged initial capital not\n
carried out=}\n
{([p]*ATER NOSTER QUI <EST< [subpuncted] ES IN CELIS &2 CETERA .(}\n

$YoYnung/vn<pr_yOy\\N+UNGE $/vn<pr_+UNGE\n
{=The origin of this word is obscure. MED follows Morris (1868) in\n
taking it to mean "adoption, election", but it is in the part of\n
the Creed that talks about God\'s only son being "begotten not made"\n
so something with the meaning "formation, shaping" might be possible.\n
Given that this scribe\'s system included substitution of thorn for\n
earlier medial and final H, it might be that this word is from a verb\n
related to OE Yo:he "clay" with the sense "form from clay". Then the\n
contrast between the creation of Adam and the begetting of Christ\n
would be drawn. I propose therefore the existence of a denominal\n
verb *Yo:hnian "to mould (from clay)"=}\n
{.}\n

$smite/vSpt13_SIRIAT\n
{=sic - clearly an error for exemplar SMAT, given the Latin PerCUSSUM\n
EST and many other places where the scribe misreads exemplar R for I,\n
or for a simple minim stroke, and vice versa. But the scribe makes\n
this same error three times (see below) and it should be considered\n
that he might have thought he was writing something other than the word\n
"smote" which made sense to him in context. In the Psalms, God\n
(via Moses) is said to "rebuke" (Ps. 105.9 increpuit) and to divide\n
(divisit Ps 135.13) it. I cannot think of any OE or ME word for\n
"rebuke" that SIRIT could plausibly be for. OE scirian is\n
possible for the meaning "divide", but SIRIAT would then have to be\n
seen as being present tense and with an unusual 3rd person ending at\n
that. The form remains a mystery, therefore, unless we assume, with\n
Sisam (1951: 113) that it is a thrice-repeated mechanical error=}\n
\'_*MOYSES\n

{=MS originally ANDELONGE with first E erased and with an attempt to\n
change O to V. The result has then been subpuncted and U written above.\n
Morris (1868) reads AND DUNGE, taking the attempt at V to be the back\n
of an overwritten D. He then conjectures that DUNGE is an error for\n
dENUNGE and suggests either "service without weariness" or (in his\n
notes) "feasting without satiety". But my suggested reading, although\n
evidently slightly fudged from an original error, is much simpler and\n
more direct, requires no emendation, and goes directly wit the other\n
senses listed with it: sight, hearing, smelling and finally touch\n
(tasting came earlier with fulness without hunger). The point is\n
that in heaven there will be fulness of life but without injury=}\n
